<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink noprint"><a href="#cite_ref-14">↑</a> </span><span class="reference-text"><a href="#AIKEN">Bernard Cohen</a>, p. 297 (2000) ; traduit de l’américain : <span class="citation not_fr_quote" lang="en">« <span class="italique">Historians of technology and computer scientists interested in history have adopted a number of qualifications that define a computer. As a result, the question of whether Mark I was or was not a computer depends not on a general consensus but rather on the particular definition that is adopted. Often, some primary defining characteristics of a computer are that it must (1) be electronic, (2) be digital (rather than analog), (3) be programmed, (4) be able to perform the four elementary operations (addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division) and -often- extract roots or obtain information from built-in tables, and (5) incorporate the principle of the stored program. A machine does not generally qualify as a computer unless it has some further properties, for example the ability to perform certain specified operations automatically in a controlled and predetermined sequence. For some historians and computer scientists, a machine must also have been actually constructed and then become fully operational.</span> »</span>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink noprint"><a href="#cite_ref-15">↑</a> </span><span class="reference-text"><abbr class="abbr indicateur-langue" title="Langue : anglais">(en)</abbr> "The introduction of punched cards into the new engine was important not only as a more convenient form of control than the drums, or because programs could now be of unlimited extent, and could be stored and repeated without the danger of introducing errors in setting the machine by hand; it was important also because it served to crystalize Babbage's feeling that he had invented something really new, something much more than a sophisticated calculating machine." <a href="#COLLIER">Bruce Collier</a>, 1970.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-on_computable_number-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink noprint"><a href="#cite_ref-on_computable_number_24-0">↑</a> </span><span class="reference-text"><span class="ouvrage" id="Turing1937"><span class="ouvrage" id="Alan_Turing1937"><abbr class="abbr indicateur-langue" title="Langue : anglais">(en)</abbr> Alan Turing, <cite class="italique" lang="en">On Computable Numbers, with an Application to the Entscheidungsproblem : Proceedings of the London Mathematical Society</cite>, London Mathematical Society, <time>1937</time> <small style="line-height:1em;">(<a href="/wiki/Digital_Object_Identifier" title="Digital Object Identifier">DOI</a> <span class="plainlinks noarchive nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://dx.doi.org/10.1112/PLMS/S2-42.1.230" rel="nofollow">10.1112/PLMS/S2-42.1.230</a></span>, <a class="external text" href="https://www.cs.virginia.edu/~robins/Turing_Paper_1936.pdf" rel="nofollow">lire en ligne</a>)</small><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=On+Computable+Numbers%2C+with+an+Application+to+the+Entscheidungsproblem&amp;rft.pub=London+Mathematical+Society&amp;rft.stitle=Proceedings+of+the+London+Mathematical+Society&amp;rft.aulast=Turing&amp;rft.aufirst=Alan&amp;rft.date=1937&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1112%2FPLMS%2FS2-42.1.230&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Ffr.wikipedia.org%3AOrdinateur"></span></span></span> et <span class="ouvrage" id="1938">« <cite style="font-style:normal;">[<i>idem</i>] : A Correction</cite> », <i>Proc. London Math. Soc.</i>, <abbr class="abbr" title="deuxième">2<sup>e</sup></abbr> série, <abbr class="abbr" title="volume">vol.</abbr> 43,‎ <time>1938</time>, <abbr class="abbr" title="pages">p.</abbr> 544-546 <small style="line-height:1em;">(<a href="/wiki/Digital_Object_Identifier" title="Digital Object Identifier">DOI</a> <span class="plainlinks noarchive nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://dx.doi.org/10.1112/plms/s2-43.6.544" rel="nofollow">10.1112/plms/s2-43.6.544</a></span>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.thocp.net/biographies/papers/turing_oncomputablenumbers_1936.pdf" rel="nofollow">lire en ligne</a>)</small><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.atitle=%5B%27%27idem%27%27%5D&amp;rft.jtitle=Proc.+London+Math.+Soc.&amp;rft.stitle=A+Correction&amp;rft.date=1938&amp;rft.volume=43&amp;rft.pages=544-546&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1112%2Fplms%2Fs2-43.6.544&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Ffr.wikipedia.org%3AOrdinateur"></span></span>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink noprint"><a href="#cite_ref-25">↑</a> </span><span class="reference-text"><a href="#AIKEN">Bernard Cohen</a>, <span class="nowrap"><abbr class="abbr" title="page(s)">p.</abbr> 66-67</span> (2000) : "Carmello Lanza…couldn't see why in the world I (Howard Aiken) wanted to do anything like this in the Physics laboratory, because we already had such a machine and nobody used it… Lanza led him up into <i>the attic</i>… There, sure enough… were the wheels that Aiken later <i>put on display in the lobby of the Computer Laboratory.</i> With them was a letter from Henry Prevost Babbage describing these wheels as part of his father's proposed calculating engine. This was the first time Aiken <i>ever heard of Babbage</i> he said, and it was this experience that led him to look up Babbage in the library and to come across his autobiography."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink noprint"><a href="#cite_ref-26">↑</a> </span><span class="reference-text"><a href="#HODGES">Andrew Hodges</a> p. 298 (1983) : <abbr class="abbr indicateur-langue" title="Langue : anglais">(en)</abbr> Without <i>conditional branching</i>, the ability to mechanize the word <i>IF</i>, the grandest calculator would be no more than a glorified adding machine. It might be thought of as assembly line, everything being laid down from start to finish, and there being no possibility of interference in the process once started.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink noprint"><a href="#cite_ref-27">↑</a> </span><span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://histoire.info.online.fr/ordinateurs.html" rel="nofollow">Les premiers ordinateurs</a>, sur online.fr, consulté le 14 octobre 2018.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink noprint"><a href="#cite_ref-28">↑</a> </span><span class="reference-text"><a href="#HODGES">Andrew Hodges</a> p. 302 (1983) : <abbr class="abbr indicateur-langue" title="Langue : anglais">(en)</abbr> Both ENIAC and Colossus were like kits out of which many slightly different machines could be made. Neither sought to embody the true universality of Babbage's conception, in which the machinery would be entirely unchanged, and only the instruction cards rewritten.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink noprint"><a href="#cite_ref-36">↑</a> </span><span class="reference-text">Une deuxième machine, dont la commande par l'<a href="/wiki/%C3%89tablissement_de_recherche_atomique_d%27Harwell" title="Établissement de recherche atomique d'Harwell">établissement de recherche atomique d'Harwell</a> fut annulée au milieu de sa construction, ne fut jamais finie par Ferranti.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink noprint"><a href="#cite_ref-37">↑</a> </span><span class="reference-text">Voir le tableau récapitulatif dans l'article : <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UNIVAC_I#Installations" title="en:UNIVAC I"><abbr class="abbr indicateur-langue" title="Langue : anglais">(en)</abbr> UNIVAC installations, 1951–1954</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink noprint"><a href="#cite_ref-38">↑</a> </span><span class="reference-text"><abbr class="abbr indicateur-langue" title="Langue : anglais">(en)</abbr> <a href="#OS">Andrew S Tanenbaum</a> page 6 (1987) "The introduction of the transistor in the mid-1950s changed the picture radically. Computers became reliable enough that they could be sold to paying customers with the expectation that they would continue to function long enough to get some useful work done."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink noprint"><a href="#cite_ref-39">↑</a> </span><span class="reference-text"><a href="#OS">Andrew S Tanenbaum</a> page 11 (1987).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink noprint"><a href="#cite_ref-40">↑</a> </span><span class="reference-text"><abbr class="abbr indicateur-langue" title="Langue : anglais">(en)</abbr> <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=WVwfDA4watU" rel="nofollow">How the Altair 8800 started the PC revolution (Part 1)</a> consulté le 12-05-2013.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink noprint"><a href="#cite_ref-41">↑</a> </span><span class="reference-text"><abbr class="abbr indicateur-langue" title="Langue : anglais">(en)</abbr> <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=pqAg0GJLPGk" rel="nofollow">Bill Gates talks about Microsoft and the Altair 8800 (1994)</a> consulté le 12-05-2013.</span>
</li>
</ol>
